MEMO: Complete QUIESCENT spectrum of AD Leo 
DATE: 10-21-03
FROM: Martin Cohen
TO: VPL team

AD Leo: spectral type: dM3e [old system], M4.5V [new]. 
Distance 4.87 pc.  

As much as possible this spectrum is assembled from empirical
spectra.  I have preferred to leave small gaps between these
spectra rather than splicing in the NextGen synthetic spectrum
whose photospheric shape is a poor fit to the optical region
and to the edges of the steam bands between the near-IR (NIR)
fragments.  Some of the original optical and spectra (received 
in digital form from their authors) were calibrated in W/m2/um
but others in ergs/cm2/s/A; IUE's native units are ergs/cm2/s/A
while my own absolute spectral codes operate in W/cm2/um. To 
avoid yet another switch I have provided the entire spectrum in 
the latter units.  To isolate any portion, e.g. the UV region, 
in ergs/cm2/s/A, one just multiplies that portion of the 
spectrum given below by 

From 0.1151-0.1797 um, the FUV spectrum is made by combining
40 "typical" IUE SW spectra; from 0.1797-0.3349 um it is from
the combination of 17 IUE LW spectra [all corrected for known
artifacts and recalibrated].  The optical spectrum in quiescence 
is that originally published by Pettersen, B.R. & Hawley, S.L.
(1989: A&A, 217, 187) from 0.3555-0.9000 um.  An independent 
red spectrum is also available from Leggett, S.K. et al. (1996:
ApJS, 104, 117) covering the range 0.5943-1.012 um.  The good
accord between the shapes of these two spectra suggests the
validity of using these to represent the optical spectrum, and
I have blended Leggett's spectrum with that sent to me by
Hawley to extend the coverage to just beyond 1.0 um. Hawley's
file was originally set to a flux scale which gives the correct
quiet UBVR fluxes when convolved with those filter curves.

Leggett et al. (1996) also offer three NIR spectra in the J, H,
and K-bands covering, respectively: 0.9656-1.3545; 1.4375-
1.8530; and 1.9952-2.4090 um.  The relevant NextGen model
(Teff=3400K, log gravity=5.0; solar metallicity) matches our
empirical optical spectrum only near its endpoints, and shows
1.4 and 1.9-um steam bands that are too deep compared with the
rather flat NIR spectra from Leggett.  These deficiencies, and
the relatively small gaps between observed fragments of 
spectrum, justify my reluctance to insert the NextGen photosphere
to link the observations.  I have experimented with a similar
NextGen model, but changing the metallicity to one-tenth solar,
and even one-hundredth, which clearly weakens the predicted
water features.  The effects at longer wavelengths on the
water features (at least those that are present within in the 
NextGen grid of models) are not profound.  Therefore, on the
advice of Leggett herself (who works with Allard who is part
of the NextGen team), I have retained the nominal photosphere
(with solar metallicity) to extrapolate beyond 2.41 um to the
FIR.  By contrast with the optical, the continuum shapes in the 
JHK windows (away from the steam bands), and their levels 
(within a few percent), are quite well-matched by this NextGen 
photosphere.

My own code simply takes this combined empirical-theoretical
shape and sets it on an absolute footing using what the
literature gives by way of UBVRIJHKL and IRAS 12-um flux density.
It is to be hoped that all these non-simultaneous observations
together nonetheless represent the expected quiescent state of
AD Leo.

NB: the Ly-alpha peak came through the IUE processing as "NaN"
so apologies for its absence.

I also have constructed the flaring spectrum of AD Leo from IUE
data but there are no equivalent spectral data in the optical 
beyond 0.46 um, nor any at all in the NIR, to the Hawley and
Leggett data.  The IUE levels are 13-16 times as high as in the
quiescent spectrum but I am unable to complete the flare 
spectrum as yet.
Martin

 Notes:
 1. Supertemplate and Kurucz model spectra are NOT tabulated at
 equal intervals of the wavelength, but rather at the wavelengths
 of the original measurements or synthetic spectra, respectively.
 2. In most cases "total uncertainty" is the appropriate error
 term to use.  It is the standard deviation of the spectral
 irradiance and includes the local and global uncertainties.
 Local and global uncertainties are given as a percentage of the
 irradiance.  Local uncertainties apply to a limited wavelength
 range, while global uncertainties effect the entire spectrum.
 Thus the global uncertainty does not contribute error to flux
 ratios or color measurements, and may, in those cases, be
 subtracted (in the RSS sense) from the total uncertainty.
